Irish Property Prices Rise, Signaling Possible Rebound for Economy; Prices Expected to Show First Annual Increase Since Housing Market Crashed
Eamon Quinn
715 words
26 September 2013
04:59
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

DUBLINIrish home prices rose for the fifth consecutive month in August, again led by a strong rise in the capital, evidence that the country is beginning to emerge from the property crash that sent its economy into a tailspin and wrecked its banks.

Ireland's government and its international bailout creditors watch home prices for signs that the economy is recovering from its banking and fiscal crisis. The banks need the property market to recover very strongly to help stem losses they suffered on their loan books after house prices collapsed more than five years ago and sent the country to the brink of bankruptcy.

The scars of the country's long property slump remain deep. Home prices countrywide are still 49% below their 2007 peak, marking one of the largest declines recorded world-wide, and it will likely be many years before they ever again match such levels.

But the Central Statistics Office data published Thursday shows prices are at last stabilizing and starting a "slow recovery," said David Duffy, a housing expert at the Economic and Social Research Institute, Ireland's leading think tank. He predicts that prices will rise 5% countrywide in 2013, the first such annual rise since the crash began, led by large gains in Dublin.

Prices rose 0.9% in August, following increases of 1.2% in July, 1.2% in June, 0.3% in May and 0.8% in April, the CSO said, and were 2.8% higher than in August 2012.

In Dublin, residential prices rose by 1.9% in August, and were 10.6% higher than a year earlier, marking the largest annual increase since April 2007.

Prices outside the capital rose in August by only 0.1% on the month, showing evidence of a clear urban/rural divide, and a very uneven housing-market recovery.

"There is no doubt that lack of supply has driven Dublin house prices up," said Alan McQuaid, chief economist at Merrion Capital.

Cash buyers are also driving price rises in the capital, which isn't a sign of a healthy housing market because there are only so many people who can pay cash for property, analysts warn.

Simon Ensor, chairman of real-estate agency Sherry FitzGerald Residential, estimates that half of the Dublin transactions are cash deals, led by expatriate Irish investors who are returning home after doing well in the U.S. and U.K.

"But the stock of houses for sale in Dublin is less than 1%, which is well below the 3% level of transactions that would characterize a healthy market," Mr. Ensor said.

A strong and sustained recovery in home prices will require a return to a sound market for home loans, but banking industry figures don't yet suggest that is under way.

Many households continue to grapple with large debts and few economists expect a very strong recovery in residential prices. The Irish government is preparing to detail next month tax increases and spending cuts in its budget for 2014, which will mark the country's sixth year of austerity.

Unemployment, though down from its peak, is at 13.4%, and emigration remains at elevated levels. Ireland's economy expanded in the three months to June by 0.4% from the first quarter, but was still 1.2% lower than a year earlier, the CSO said last week.

Nonetheless, the evidence of rising house price rises should come as a relief to the coalition government as a sign that the wider economy is starting to recover.

In the final years of the housing boom, 2006 and 2007, prices rose 14.7% and 7.3% respectively.

When the bubble burst, Irish home prices fell by an annual 7% in 2008, and then plunged 18.8% in 2009 and 12.4% in 2010. Prices also posted double-digit declines in 2011 and 2012.

With prices for commercial property also slumping, Irish banks were left with huge losses, and required huge sums from Irish taxpayers to rescue them from collapse. Ireland eventually was forced to strike an international bailout deal in late 2010.

U.S. News: Wealth Rebound Continues --- Rising Rates, Fiscal Discord Threaten Households' Postrecession Run of Prosperity
By Ben Casselman
865 words
26 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A6
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Americans' household finances have made up most of the ground lost in the recession, but rising interest rates and renewed turmoil in Washington are threatening to short-circuit that recovery.

Rebounding home prices and a rising stock market helped boost household wealth by more than $1.3 trillion in the second quarter of this year, Federal Reserve data showed Wednesday. The gain marked the seventh consecutive quarterly increase and pushed household net worth -- the value of homes, stocks and other assets minus debts and other liabilities -- to $74.8 trillion, an all-time high. Adjusting for inflation, net worth is about 4% below its peak, meaning households have made back about 80% of what they lost during the bust.

The data echo other recent signs that households are emerging from their prolonged postcrisis slump. After years of deleveraging -- both voluntarily, through paying down debts, and involuntarily, through foreclosures and bankruptcies -- the share of Americans' paychecks that goes to debt payments stands near a 30-year low. That has given households more money to spend on everything from cars to vacations: Average consumer spending surpassed its prerecession high in 2012, before adjusting for inflation.

That recovery has been a key driver of economic growth during a period of government cutbacks and lackluster business investment. Economic output would actually have contracted in both late 2012 and early 2013 if it hadn't been for solid gains in consumer spending. Many economists expect the government to revise upward its estimate of second-quarter spending growth when new figures are released Thursday.

But it is unclear whether that momentum can continue. Job growth remains weak and may even have slowed modestly over the summer; many economists expect next week's monthly employment report to show little if any acceleration in hiring in September. Income gains have been muted and concentrated among wealthier Americans. Income for the typical household was flat in 2012, after adjusting for inflation, and remains well below precrisis highs.

Federal Reserve policy makers have hinted that the uncertain consumer outlook is one reason for their decision last week to leave unchanged their $85 billion-a-month bond-buying program. "The drivers of consumer spending do not look particularly supportive," New York Fed President William Dudley said in a speech Tuesday.

Another wild card: Washington, where the latest budget battle is threatening to lead to a government shutdown next week and a fight over raising the congressionally mandated borrowing limit could roil financial markets. Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke cited the issues as another reason for last week's decision to keep stimulus efforts in place.

One key factor offsetting both weak income growth and Washington turmoil has been the rebound in the housing market. Home prices rose at their fastest pace in more than seven years in July, according to the Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller index, and new-home sales rebounded in August after dropping sharply in July. Rising real-estate values accounted for nearly half of the gains in household wealth in the second quarter.

Linda Miner, a real-estate agent in Vail, Colo., said home sales were strong this summer, especially at the high and low end of the market. But she said that with prices still down from before the housing bust, sellers aren't experiencing the windfalls that might once have led them to spend big on renovations or furnishings when they moved into a new home.

Joe Blair and his wife sold their Vail-area home last fall for about $30,000 less than they'd paid for it in 2005. After several months of searching, they paid about $600,000 for a new home in the area in July. Despite trading up to a larger home to accommodate their three young children, the Blairs held on to their old furniture. Their one splurge, Mr. Blair said, was on new patio furniture he found at Home Depot for 60% off. If not for the sale, Mr. Blair said, "we'd be hitting the garage sales."

Economists fear the recent spike in interest rates could slow the housing rebound. Mortgage rates are up nearly a full percentage point since May, and were even higher before the Fed's bond-buying announcement last week. Rising rates, combined with higher prices, are making homes less affordable: The National Association of Realtors' housing affordability index is down more than 25% since January.

Rising rates haven't yet dented prices, although many economists expect that to change as August data become available. But higher rates are already bringing an end to the three-year refinancing boom that has helped millions of borrowers free up cash for other uses. The mortgage company Freddie Mac estimates that borrowers who refinanced in the second quarter will save more than $6 billion in interest over the next year.

"It's a real shot in the arm in terms of supporting both consumer spending and investment spending," Freddie Mac chief economist Frank Nothaft said. "We'll see a lot less refinancing going forward."

Kyle W. Blackmon, who represented Mr. Weill, has the River House listing, along with another Brown Harris Stevens Broker, John Burger, who has sold many of New York's most expensive apartments.

U.S. News: Home Prices Jump, but Pace May Slow
By Nick Timiraos
544 words
25 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

U.S. home prices rose by their fastest pace in more than seven years in July, according to an index released Tuesday, though more-recent data suggest price gains could soon moderate.

Prices in 20 major U.S. cities increased 12.4% in July compared with the same month last year, according to the Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller index.

Home-price inflation has accelerated sharply over the past year as more buyers have chased a shrinking supply of houses for sale. Fewer properties have been selling out of foreclosure, and until May, mortgage rates had hovered near record lows, which let buyers qualify for slightly more debt without significantly increasing their monthly payment.

Rising mortgage rates, which are up nearly one percentage point since May, could ultimately test buyers' willingness to pay more. That may have accelerated some purchases from buyers who had initially planned to buy later this year, but it has made other buyers more hesitant.

Real-estate agents and executives have reported slowdowns in sales traffic in August and September. Also, there are signs that investors have begun to step back from the market amid higher prices.

Data releases expected on Wednesday for new-home sales in August and on Thursday for contracts to buy existing homes should provide more up-to-date gauges of demand.

Tuesday's report hinted at a possible slowdown in the rate at which prices are going up. While all 20 cities tracked by the Case-Shiller index gained in July, the pace at which prices rose slowed in 15 cities.

"The slowing in monthly gains is not a nail in the recovery's coffin," said Bill Banfield, director of capital markets at Quicken Loans. "In fact it shows a normalizing of the market and that this growth can be sustained."

Home prices rose 1.8% in July from June. While that was more sluggish than the month-over-month increases in the previous three months, home prices tend to rise fastest in the spring, and they typically peak in June.

The gain in July was still the largest for that month since the Case-Shiller index began its count in 2000.

The index tracks home prices on a three-month moving average. Tuesday's report measured prices on home sales that were recorded in the May-to-July period, and buyers would have entered into contracts to buy those homes one or two months before then.

Prices are up most sharply in many of the Western and Southwestern markets that saw some of the biggest declines. Prices have jumped 27.5% in Las Vegas over the past year, while San Francisco's have gained 24.8% and Los Angeles's 20.8%.

Rising mortgage rates could make it more difficult for sellers to keep asking buyers to pay more, especially in markets that have witnessed some of the sharpest gains. Home builders have been particularly aggressive over the past year raising prices.

Now, rising mortgage rates have been "almost like a red light on the frantic price inflation," said Ivy Zelman, chief executive of Zelman & Associates, a research and advisory firm.

(WSJ October 2, 2013)

(END)

Western Alliance Bancorp. got a pleasant surprise in May when it sold a vacant store in Victorville, Calif. out of its foreclosed-property portfolio.

The Phoenix-based bank had internally marked down the value of the 30,000-square-foot property, which fell into default in June 2012, to $972,000 from the $1.2 million value of the loan. Western Alliance was actually able to book a profit by selling it for $1.1 million to David Hong, who plans to use it to house his clothing-embroidery company.

"As real estate values have risen, along with demand from buyers, banks are in the better position to dispose of their OREO," Robert Sarver, Western Alliance's chief executive, said in an interview, referring to Other Real Estate Owned, the term the banking industry uses for foreclosed properties.

Rising commercial property values throughout the country have enabled banks to cleanse themselves of the mountains of distressed office buildings, shopping centers, hotels and other real estate that they reluctantly took over during the financial downturn. That has helped banks return to health and transferred thousands of properties to new investors who have breathed new life into them.

What is even more important is what didn't happen. As delinquent loans and OREO swelled, there was widespread fear among regulators that commercial real estate would follow residential real estate off the cliff and deliver another massive blow to the economy. In 2010, bad commercial-real-estate assets hit a postcrisis peak of $146 billion, according to debt research firm Trepp LLC.

Banks posted huge losses and dozens failed due to bad commercial real-estate loans made during the boom years. But the pain wasn't nearly as bad as expected, in large part because banks didn't sell their troubled loans and foreclosed properties during the darkest days of the downturn and values bounced back faster than many expected, banking and real-estate experts say.

Banks have whittled down the volume of bad loans and OREO to $60 billion by selling assets or working out deals with borrowers, according to Trepp. For commercial OREO alone, the five largest U.S. banks have reduced their holdings to $1.7 billion in the second quarter, down from $3.5 billion in mid-2010, according to the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. "I sleep a lot easier today," says Julie Stackhouse, a vice president at the Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis, which has examined the holdings of banks' foreclosed properties.

Meanwhile, in the real-estate industry, the steady sale by banks of the troubled assets has boosted a new class of developers and investors. While moguls and institutions have been on the other side of most high-profile distressed sales of assets such as Worldwide Plaza in Manhattan, the great majority of properties sold have been purchased by mom-and-pop investors, immigrants and other smaller players, property brokers say.

Mr. Hong, who bought the store in the Victorville strip shopping center, is the 36-year old son of South Korean immigrants. His manufacturing company, X-Treme Stitching, which specializes in baseball hats, will take half the space and the other half will be sublet to retailers, he says.

Banks are the largest holders of commercial real-estate debt, with about $1.1 trillion of the total $2.1 trillion in commercial mortgages, according to the Federal Reserve. Many decided in the early years of the crisis not to sell OREO and distressed loans because they wanted to avoid a repeat of the early 1990s, when lenders dumped assets at huge discounts only to see them rebound in value in a few years.

Their strategy this time to hold and extend troubled loans rather than sell was derided by critics as "extend and pretend." But now many who raised red flags at the time say the practice looks smart in hindsight.

"It's worked a lot better than I would have guessed," said Arthur Segel, a real-estate professor at Harvard Business School.

U.S. banks wrote down the value of their commercial real-estate holdings by $134.5 billion between 2007 and 2012, according to the Mortgage Bankers Association. But banks are getting better prices as the real-estate market has improved. For example, in 2009, lenders were taking 70% to 80% losses when selling risky assets like raw land, according to Matthew Anderson, a managing director at Trepp. Today that discount is in the 30% to 40% range, he says.

Bank selling also has picked up in the past few years as values have rebounded. U.S. commercial-property values have increased 42% since hitting their postcrash trough in 2009, according to the Moody's/REAL Commercial Property Price Indices.

Of course, for commercial loans, the problem hasn't gone away. The cache of OREO and delinquent loans is still far larger than historic levels -- it was $13.6 billion in the first quarter of 2007 for U.S. banks, according to Trepp.

Many community banks in particular continue to struggle, and banks with less than $1 billion in assets have cut their OREO and delinquent loans by only 40% since 2010, according to Trepp.

The steady sale of OREO and troubled loans has helped fuel a virtuous cycle within the commercial-property markets. New owners are reinvesting in buildings, helping boost the value of surrounding properties.

A set of 16 condominium town houses in La Mesa, Calif., sat vacant for more than four years, after Bank of America Corp. foreclosed on the nearly-completed project in 2008.

In May, investment group Pathfinder Partners LLC bought the property for $3.1 million. After the floors were fixed and lighting fixtures replaced, the town homes are being rented now. Pathfinder co-founder Lorne Polger says he is on track to eventually sell the units for two-thirds more than he paid for them.

Australia's Banks Warned on Mortgage Lending; Low Rates Risk Fueling Surge in Speculative Home Buying
James Glynn
681 words
25 September 2013
01:13
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SYDNEYAustralia's central bank advised the nation's lenders against being too keen to offer mortgages to their customers, saying record low interest rates risked fueling an economically damaging surge in speculative home buying.

In its routine review of financial stability, the central bank said lenders should maintain the highest standards when offering mortgages, as house prices continue to recover from a long period of stagnation.

The low interest-rate setting combined with loose mortgage lending may end up boosting property speculation, it said, posing a risk to the economy if house prices were to drop sharply.

Government figures show the number of mortgages approved by lenders has risen each month so far this year. Still, the central bank refrained from referring to the accompanying rise in house prices across the country as a bubble.

"Lending practices in the residential mortgage market will be an important area to watch," the central bank said in its twice-yearly review of the health of banks and household debt. "A sustained period of below-average interest rates could increase speculative activity in the housing market and encourage marginal borrowers to increase debt."

The International Monetary Fund has called on regulators to act to prevent house prices from rising too steeply, recalling the experience of 2008 when reckless mortgage lending triggered the global financial crisis.

The Reserve Bank of Australia's burgeoning concern over house values has made further immediate rate cuts that could fuel further price gains less likely, some economists say.

"The strength of the property market and the comments today raise the bar for further rate cuts," said Paul Brennan, chief economist at Citigroup in Sydney.

The central bank's cautious language comes amid anxiety that the sharp rebound in Australian home values this year has been driven to a significant extent by property investors--particularly in more cosmopolitan cities like Sydney.

Australian house prices rose more than 5% from a year earlier in the second quarter, the fastest pace since 2010, while auction clearance rates and home-loan approvals have also risen, turning the housing market into a rare bright spot in an economy that is slowing as a long mining boom cools.

The central bank has lowered the official cash-rate target eight times in almost two years to help spur nonmining parts of the country's resources-dominated economy. It left the rate at a record low 2.5% at its last policy meeting, on Sept. 3, and hasn't ruled out further reductions.

In its review Wednesday, the central bank said lenders needed to resist the temptation to ease borrowing standards as demand for mortgages climbed and competition in the loan market increased.

"It is important they do not respond to pressures to boost revenue by imprudently loosening their lending standards, or by making ill-considered moves into new markets or products," it said.

National Australia Bank Ltd., one of the nation's top four lenders, said it was remaining "cautious" in its approach to mortgage lending. The Australian Bankers' Association, an industry group, said there was nothing in the central bank's remarks to indicate concern over current lending practices.

"Banks are lending responsibly," said Tony Burke, acting chief executive of the lobby group. "There's no doubt home lending is a competitive marketplace, but we're confident banks are lending responsibly because problems aren't emerging."

The central bank cautioned home buyers, too, not to have unrealistic expectations about the scope for future house-price rises. It added that households appeared to be steering clear of excessive debt levels.

"Indicators of household stress are low," it said in the review, but added it was concerned an increasing number of property investors were being advised to finance new home purchases with their retirement savings.

"This type of investment strategy is being heavily promoted," the central bank said, adding that it risked pushing up speculative demand for property.

Ahead of the Tape: Homebuilder Stocks Look Expensive
By Spencer Jakab
331 words
23 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
22
English
(c) 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

When the Federal Reserve showed itself to be a taper tiger last week, one of the sharpest reactions came from U.S. homebuilding stocks. For example, KB Home and Lennar Corp., which both report quarterly earnings Tuesday, leapt by 9.6% and 6.6% in the last two hours of the trading session, respectively.

Lower rates for longer clearly are positive for companies that sell people the most expensive purchase of their lives on credit. Even so, there are plenty of other positive factors for homebuilders, from rising prices to increasingly compelling demographics.

The problem is that these appear to have been priced in well before the prospect of less Fed bond buying and rising interest rates appeared. Despite headline after headline about rising home prices and even bidding wars, not a single major homebuilder has outperformed the broad U.S. market so far this year. A number of them, including PulteGroup Inc. and Hovnanian Enterprises Inc., are actually lower.

Profits are rising for homebuilders, of course. KB is seen earning 21 cents a share for the period through August, compared with four cents a year earlier, and Lennar is seen earning 45 cents versus 40 cents, according to FactSet. But earnings will have to get a lot better to compensate for stock gains before 2013.

Lennar now trades at 14.8 times the next 12 months' projected earnings and KB is at 17.6 times. In the seven Septembers prior to the bursting of the housing bubble, they traded at 7.62 and 6.57 times on average, respectively. Even on projected 2016 earnings, they appear pricey.

Barring a much more substantial rise in mortgage rates, the underlying impetus for the housing recovery remains intact.Construction and home sales probably have a long runway ahead of them. Just don't bet the house on it.

U.S. News: Mortgage Worries Spur Home Buying
By Sarah Portlock
564 words
20 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- Sales of previously owned homes rose unexpectedly in August to the highest level since 2007, as buyers rushed to lock in deals before mortgage rates increased further.

But the industry's trade group warned that sales could slow in the coming months amid higher borrowing costs and home prices.

Existing-home sales rose 1.7% in August from a month earlier to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 5.48 million, the National Association of Realtors said Thursday. That was the best month of sales since February 2007, when the housing bubble was just starting to burst. The pace was 13.2% higher than the same period last year.

"This is partly due to the rise in interest rates, which again hurries some of the people into making the decision," said Lawrence Yun, chief economist for the Realtors' group. August is a lagging indicator of activity in June and July, when rates began their ascent, he said. Economists had predicted sales would fall to a pace of 5.25 million in August.

The figure "is especially encouraging as it suggests that the housing-market recovery is not showing any ill-effects from the recent surge in mortgage rates -- at least not yet," said Millan Mulraine of TD Securities.

Still, Mr. Yun warned the surging home-sales pace could be the "last hurrah" for the next 12 to 18 months as rising rates and home prices start to affect affordability and hold back buyers. Already, real-estate agents are seeing a decline in house tours, Mr. Yun said.

A strong housing market has buoyed the economic recovery by improving confidence among consumers, encouraging household spending and generating construction jobs.

Rising mortgage rates this summer have sparked a rush to buy homes as prospective homeowners want to lock in rates before they climb further. The average rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage was 4.5% this week, Freddie Mac said Thursday. That level, while historically low, is well above spring levels of around 3.5%.

Rates have been increasing in recent months as speculation grew that the Federal Reserve would start winding down its $85-billion-a-month bond-buying program, which was designed in part to push down long-term interest rates. But Wednesday, the central bank said it would keep its program in place as it awaits more evidence that the economic recovery will be sustained.

Other reports have indicated a slower pace of sales of new homes as buyers adjust to higher rates. Contracts to buy new homes fell by 13.4% in July from June, and some builders and analysts have reported softness in new-home orders during August.

If rising mortgage rates are accelerating purchases by some buyers that had been sitting on the fence, that could benefit existing homes over new homes because buyers can't lock in a mortgage rate on a home that is under construction.

The Realtors group said Thursday the median price of an existing home was $212,100, up 14.7% from a year earlier, its strongest gain since October 2005. And inventories of previously owned homes remain tight. The number of homes available for sale in August was 2.25 million, up slightly from July but down 6% from a year ago.

LONDONBritain's resurgent property market has led to an improvement in the government's finances. As the number of transactions climbs, so does the revenue from taxes on those transactions.

This is the first indication that the economic recovery is helping Treasury chief George Osborne's task of narrowing the country's budget deficit, which remains one of the highest in Europe.

Stamp Duty Land Taxwhich is charged in the U.K. on property purchases over 125,000 ($201,000)has hit its highest level since the financial crisis hit in 2008, data from the tax department, HM Revenue and Customs shows.

In July, stamp duty totaled 886 million203 million higher than the same month last year. The latest figure for August shows stamp duty revenue remains buoyant at 803 million. In comparison, at the height of the recession in 2009, stamp duty plunged to 200 million per month as the property market stalled.

The increase in stamp duty was highlighted in public finance data, released Friday, that showed the budget deficit was 13.2 billion in August, lower than the 14.4 billion borrowed in the same month last year, according to the Office for National Statistics.

In recent months, economic data have indicated a recovery is beginning to take hold in the U.K. As the economy strengthens it should lead to an improvement in public finances as tax revenues from companies, sales and employment boost government coffers, while the amount the government has to pay in welfare payments such as unemployment benefits declines.

An ONS statistician Friday said the rise in stamp duty receipts was the first evidence of this improvement.

This should aid Mr. Osborne's task of tackling the county's budget deficit, which is forecast to be 6.8% of gross domestic product this year, one of the largest in Europe.

Martin Beck, U.K. economist at Capital Economics, said Friday's data suggests that the economic recovery could be finally starting to make its presence felt in the borrowing figures.

"With signs that economic growth is accelerating, the public sector deficit could well be on course to decline more rapidly than the Office for Budget Responsibility [the government budget watchdog] expects," he said.

The increased stamp duty take is the latest evidence showing a revival of the U.K. property market, largely as a result of government-sponsored programs to support demand.

Official figures released Tuesday show that the average U.K. house price was 3.3% higher in July than in the same month last year. A gauge that measures house prices in England, the largest part of the U.K., is now above its precrisis peak of January 2008.

The rise in property prices has ignited fears the U.K. is at risk of inflating a new property bubble, as demand for homes has yet to be matched by a significant increase in supply.

The fiercest criticism has been directed at the government's Help-to-Buy program, which offers cheap loans for home buyers struggling to find the cash for a down payment and taxpayer guarantees to lower the cost of mortgages.

One company benefiting from the revival in the property market is U.K. estate agent Foxtons Group PLC, which Friday priced its initial public offering at the top of its range and will begin trading with a market value of about 649 million.

Foxtons, which has 40 branches in the London area, said the IPO was priced at 230 pence a share and would raise 390 million.

The company was bought by private-equity firm BC Partners in 2007 for 360 million. Foxtons is the second major U.K. estate agent to list its share publicly this year after Countrywide PLC, which raised 200 million in March.

Rebecca Howard

English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WELLINGTON, New ZealandEconomists have long focused on dairy prices when gauging the health of agriculture-rich New Zealand. When a quake destroyed the country's second-largest city in 2011, they turned their eyes to heavy equipment and building materials.

Now, economists are starting to more pay attention to another indicator: household items like the latest liquid-crystal-display televisions and sofas. New Zealand's gathering economic momentum is creating a feel good factor among consumers that is adding to expectations the country will be among the first developed nations to hike interest rates since the global financial crisis.

New Zealand's economy expanded by 2.5% on-year in the second quarter, official figures published Thursday showed. Economists had expected the economy to expand by 2.2%, hurt by a drought earlier in the year that hampered production in the country's dominant dairy industry.

Spending by households in the three months through June on durable goods like TVs was the largest in more than six years, Statistics New Zealand said. Economists took that as a signal that the economic recovery was spreading beyond sectors like housing and construction.

Des Flynn, a customer services manager at national retailer The Warehouse Group Ltd., which sells everything from big screen TVs to clothing and furniture, said there is been a "steady increase" in consumer spending over the last few months, with TV sales especially strong across all brands, including international name-brands like Sony and the retailer's own brand. "More people are buying and more people are buying more," he said.

Consumer confidence is around a three-year high, house prices are rising and unemployment remains below levels seen last year.

In the second quarter, the economy expanded only 0.2% but it was a "remarkable number when you consider that the agriculturally-leveraged economy of New Zealand was in the midst of one of its worst droughts on record," said Stephen Toplis, head of research at Bank of New Zealand.

While agricultural output was down 6.4% on the quarter "households, buoyed by rising house prices, increasing employment, ultralow interest rates, and modest real wage growth are spending up large," Mr. Toplis said.

The government in March declared a drought across the entire North Island and parts of the South Island, saying it could shave more than 2 billion New Zealand dollars (US$1.7 billion) off the NZ$200 billion economy. Still, a construction boom led by Christchurch, where a string of earthquakes since early 2011 have caused more than US$34 billion in damage, has helped fuel activity in previously weak parts of the economy like retail sales.

"Aside from drought-induced volatility, the New Zealand economy is firmly in the grip of a widespread upturn," said Westpac Chief Economist Dominick Stephens.

House prices in Auckland, a city experiencing rapid population growth, rose 13% on-year in August, while in Christchurch, prices rallied 11% over the same period amid an ongoing housing shortage. Nationwide prices are up 8.5% on-year. That is flowing through to service industries like architecture firms and realtors.

"We haven't seen this level of activity for a long time," said Lisa Hinton, a practice director of Context Architects, which has offices in Auckland and Christchurch. Record-low interest rates are spurring residential construction, she said, adding that the firm plans to add three staff to meet growing demand.

New Zealand's central bank has held its policy interest rate steady for more than two years, but rising costs of housing and construction may force it to act before the end of the year.

Nick Tuffley, chief economist for ASB Bank, said the Reserve Bank of New Zealand may hold off until March next year but "the risks are skewed to a slightly earlier or more aggressive start" given heat in the housing sector.

New Zealand experienced a housing boom in the early 2000s, fueled by easy credit and a local preference for investing in the housing sector. At the time, the central bank tried to moderate house prices by boosting the cash rate, with little success. With so much of the country's wealth tied up in housing, when prices eventually did fall the nation dipped into a recession even before the global financial crisis.

Sydney-based Royal Bank of Canada economist Michael Turner said that while growth was starting to spread beyond the construction sector, "there is not much sign of inflation at the moment and the currency is still fairly strong" which will likely keep the cash rate unchanged for now.

It was the seventh straight month of price increases compared with year-earlier levels, data released Wednesday by the National Bureau of Statistics showed.

Month-on-month price gains also picked up, after moderating for the previous four months, signaling limited impact of the government's tightening measures.

The central government has been tightening property-sector controls for nearly four years to rein in surging house prices, restricting second-home purchases and slowing the flow of credit to developers. But Beijing is also concerned that excessive curbs on the housing sector could derail the country's fragile economic recovery.

Liu Jianwei, a statistician from the statistics bureau, said in a separate commentary that the jump in year-over-year prices was partly due to a lower base of comparison and that the sequential gains in new-home prices have moderated since March.

"The statistics bureau's continued effort to paint a picture of still-benign housing-price conditions may imply that the central government wants to deal with other issues first before making a very clear stand," said Jinsong Du, a Credit Suisse analyst.

He said this indicates that new tightening measures, particularly national curbs, are unlikely to be rolled out in the near term, though may come after a meeting of senior Communist Party officials in November, where leaders are expected to discuss key policy objectives.

Analysts and government researchers have called for more sustainable measures, such as land overhaul and property taxes, to rein in the real-estate market, rather than relying on moves such as limits on home purchases.

Entrenched imbalances in the housing market, such as strong demand in major cities and oversupply in smaller cities, as well as local governments' reliance on revenue from land sales, have complicated Beijing's efforts on home prices.

Prices in August rose an average 7.5% from a year earlier, compared with 6.7% in July, calculations by The Wall Street Journal showed. Prices were up an average 0.79% month-over-month in August after a 0.68% gain in July.

Prices of new homes in 66 of the 70 large and medium-size cities surveyed rose in August from July. In July, prices rose in 62 cities. Year-on-year, prices were up in 69 cities in August, the same as July's tally.

Average home prices in large cities such as Beijing, Shanghai, Shenzhen and Guangzhou recorded strong on-year gains in August. Beijing prices rose 14.9%, above the 14.1% gain in July, and Shanghai prices advanced 15.4% from a 13.7% increase. In Guangzhou, prices were up 18.8%, accelerating from the 17.2% gain the previous month and in Shenzhen, average home prices rose 18.1%, picking up from a 16.6% rise in July.

While developers may be pleased with the sales increases, they are wary of rising land costs. The land market in major cities has seen breakneck price appreciation in recent months as companies aggressively buy more land. The record prices have caused them to take a longer-term view on their operations, holding on to already-developed areas and shifting away from a purely "build-and-sell" model.

"We're adopting a more sustainable model of property development, including tourism elements such as having hot springs in our projects, which will add value to our property," said Tracy Wong, vice president of Rong Hui Group, a closely held developer in Fuzhou. "Now with scarcity of land, we can't just depend on sales and can't keep replenishing our land bank."

Home building creates jobs as well as demand for a broad array of building materials and household goods. The sector's growth, however, has been held back by supply constraints as the industry emerges from its long slump.

Fed officials could announce a decision to start the pullback at the end of a two-day meeting later Wednesday.

Higher rates appear to be tempering optimism in the sector. The steady rise of home-builder confidence paused in September, according to a gauge released Tuesday by the National Association of Home Builders. The NAHB said its index remains at its highest level since November 2005, but many builders are "reporting some hesitancy on the part of buyers due to the sharp increase in interest rates."

Recent reports from the government and the National Association of Realtors showed declines in housing sales in July, while the rapid run-up in prices has started to ease.

Home prices rose 12.1% for the year ended in June, according to the S&P/Case-Shiller index, which tracks prices in 20 major U.S. metro areas, down slightly from a gain of 12.2% for the 12 months ended in May.

Still, home builders and economists say more homes are needed to meet rising demand driven by a gradually improving labor market and interest rates that remain historically low.

"We look for [home-] start activity to continue to move broadly higher in coming months as rising prices and lean inventories boost demand," said Barclays economist Cooper Howes. "We expect that residential investment will provide a positive contribution to GDP growth in coming quarters despite the recent month-to-month volatility in start."

In January it said it would take at least three years to reach that goal. Still, getting hold of bricks and tiles takes twice as long as it did before Help to Buy.

Galliford Try, another builder, said it is having to pay bricklayers up to 20% more than it was six months ago.

"What would have been a gradual increase in unit numbers is instead a shock, so house builders are struggling to come to grips with that," said Chief Executive Greg Fitzgerald.

Mr. Cable has questioned whether the second phase of the Help-To-Buy program due to start next year should go ahead. On Monday he suggested the government should look into restricting it to specific areas of the country. Much of the increase in house prices has been fueled by London and southeast while the market in other parts of Britain has been more sluggish.

Underscoring the growing concerns over the risk of a new housing bubble, the Royal Institute of Chartered Surveyors trade body said on Friday that the Bank of England should use its powers to prevent house prices in the U.K. rising by more than 5% a year. Firmly anchored house price expectations would limit excessive risk taking and help limit an unsustainable rise in debt, it said.

BOE Governor Mark Carney has said that aside from some pockets, housing market activity in the U.K. remains below precrisis levels.

"We do need to be vigilant about this, but we also need to look at the starting point," Mr. Carney told a parliamentary committee Thursday. "There are big pockets in the country where there has not been any meaningful recovery in the housing market."

Looking ahead to the next general election in 2015, Mr. Alexander said his party was committed to keeping the coalition alive until the election is held.

On Monday, Mr. Cable also said it was "certainly possible" that the Lib Dems and the Conservatives could go their separate ways a few months before the election, which is due in 2015, a rare comment on an issue ministers seek to avoid.

"It's our intention as a party to make sure this coalition sees out its full term," Mr. Alexander said. "The last thing you do as a party of government is walk away with a few months to go."

Mr. Alexander said there were good early signs that the economy was recovering in a balanced way.

"But also it's very early days and there is a lot more work to be done to make sure that it is a lasting recovery. So I don't think there's any sense... of complacency, that we can take our foot of the gas. Quite the opposite," he said.

The U.K. economy expanded by 0.7% in the second quarter and in recent weeks a run of strong economic data and surveys have indicated that the recovery is gathering pace. The Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development said this month that it expects economic growth in the U.K. to outpace the U.S., Germany, France and Japan in the second half of 2013.

In new guidance, the Bank of England has said it would not consider changing its ultralow interest rates or reversing its additional monetary stimulus program until the U.K. unemployment rate falls to 7%, which it forecasts won't happen until 2016. The latest official figures show the unemployment rate was 7.7% in July.

However, in a sign that investors are more bullish about the economic outlook, financial markets are pricing in an interest rate rise in the second quarter of 2015.

"We broadened slightly the bank's mandate earlier this year and the bank is rightly operating within the wider flexibility that they have using tools that are used in other parts of the world," Mr. Alexander said. "It seems to me that markets respond rationally to those signals and that's the whole basis on which the bank is operating its policy."

"People should believe what the Bank of England says, I certainly do," he said, declining further comment on monetary policy.

The rising cost of living is fast becoming a key political battleground after several years in which inflation has outpaced wage growth. Mr. Alexander said he hoped workers would benefit as firms start to grow, but the government was also helping by raising the income tax threshold to 10,000, a level Lib Dems say they want to raise further in the next parliament.

Mr. Alexander is due to give a speech at his party conference later in which he will say taxes on the wealthy will be one of the Lib Dems' central promises for the next parliament. He explained that the party would be targeting accumulated wealth rather than earnings and it didn't have a set definition for wealthy people in salary terms.

One of the party's key tax offerings at the next general election is likely to be is long-standing plan for a so-called "mansion tax"a 1% levy on the value of homes valued at more than 2 milliona measure they say could raise about 2 billion a year but which is opposed by the Conservatives, who say it would be expensive and ineffective.

On Monday the Lib Dems accidentally sent to reporters an internal document for lawmakers' eyes only that said it was looking at how those earning more than 50,000 ($79,328) a year could make a "further contribution" in taxes. The party quickly said it was a mistake and not its policy.

"We have no plans to increase any of the rates of income tax. None of the income-tax rates will go up under the Liberal Democrats," Mr. Alexander said. "So on the value of a home over 2 million you would pay a bit extra, and we would reduce the tax relief on pension contributions. The Liberals basically believe in taxing work, effort and income less and accumulations of wealth, particularly unearned wealth, more."

U.S. News: Home-Sales Pace Eases As Prices, Rates Climb
By Nick Timiraos and Conor Dougherty
1088 words
17 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
9
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

After a yearlong rally, the U.S. housing market is showing signs of cooling as higher prices and interest rates, a slowdown in investor purchases and shortages of homes for sale weigh on one of the economy's brightest sectors.

While few economists and industry watchers believe the housing recovery will stall, there is growing evidence that the exuberance that prompted bidding wars and led to double-digit price gains is easing. Redfin, an online real-estate brokerage, said its agents had multiple bids on 61% of its homes in August, down from 76% in March.

"It's clear there will be some moderation in demand," said Lawrence Yun, chief economist for the National Association of Realtors. He noted that the use of electronic "lockboxes" used by listing agents, an indicator of foot traffic at homes on the market, showed a "measurable decline" during August.

Some closely watched measures of housing activity, including sales of previously owned homes, may not yet capture the full extent of any slowdown, in part because they measure sales that went under contract earlier in the summer when activity was still robust. The Realtors group is set to report Thursday on existing home sales for August, which will show completed sales of homes that went into contracts one to two months earlier.

The consensus of economists surveyed last week by Dow Jones Newswires estimates that the pace of sales fell to a 5.24 million seasonally adjusted annual rate in August, down about 3% from July but ahead of last year's 4.84 million.

Recent reports by some of the nation's biggest new-home builders look worse. Hovnanian Enterprises Inc. last week blamed higher home prices and interest rates on a 12.7% decline in new orders during July and August versus those months last year. "In hindsight, we might have been a little bit aggressive with our price increases," said Ara Hovnanian, the company's chief executive, on an investor call.

New-home sales declined 4% in August from July, according to a survey by John Burns Real Estate Consulting. New-home orders rose by just 1% from a year earlier, compared with year-to-year gains of 11% in July and 25% during the second quarter, according to research firm Zelman & Associates.

Mortgage rates have climbed more than a full percentage point over the past four months, to around 4.8% last week for the average 30-year fixed-rate mortgage from 3.6% in May, as investors predict that the Federal Reserve will wind down measures that had pushed mortgage rates to rock-bottom levels.

Some real-estate agents believe new homes will be hurt more than existing homes because builders have been more aggressive in raising prices on new homes and because buyers can't close quickly on homes that haven't been built.

Buyers "want to lock something in before the rate gets away from them," said Brett Dickinson, a real-estate agent with Sotheby's International Realty in San Diego.

To be sure, there is often a slowdown in real-estate sales between the summer and fall, as school starts up and families look to stay put. And some real-estate agents welcome a slowdown because it eases their fears that prices were rising too fast.

Some agents say the biggest problem in the market is "seller greed" -- that is, sellers pricing their homes too high, said Jim Klinge, a real-estate agent in Carlsbad, Calif. Faced with rising rates, buyers aren't going for higher prices. "They don't realize our 12- to 18-month full-tilt boogie is over," he said.

While higher rates and prices are knocking marginal buyers out of the market, others, like Ghalib and Nancy Wahidi, will remain in the market but look for a less expensive house. The Wahidis' original price range topped out at $1.2 million, but they reduced it after rates went up, said Dr. Wahidi, a 38-year-old physician. They are set to close this week on a four-bedroom $1.03 million home in Ladera Ranch, Calif., around 4% below its original list price.

Sarah Withers and her husband accelerated their decision to buy a home in August after rates increased. She said they are willing to pay $1,000 to get out of their apartment lease, which runs through next April. Last week, they signed a contract to buy a three-bedroom townhouse for $115,000. After the house sat on the market for a month, the sellers reduced the price, drawing four offers.

"We don't believe that the recent increases in mortgage rate are going to in any way, shape or form snuff out the housing recovery," said Timothy Sloan, chief financial officer at Wells Fargo & Co., at an investor conference last week. It wasn't logical, he added, to expect double-digit price gains to last forever.

Still, any slowdown in home construction could take a bite out of the economy's already slow growth rate. Steve Capen, a real-estate broker with Keller Williams Realty in St. Petersburg, Fla., had planned on hiring another agent or assistant this year. "Now, there wouldn't be a need for it," he said. "We have to work harder to find the buyers."

To some extent, the housing market has become a victim of its surprising success. The inventory of existing homes for sale has plunged to a 4.8-month supply in July from twice that in July 2010, according to the Realtors' group. Inventory has risen six straight months even after adjusting for seasonal factors, according to Trulia, a real-estate listings service, but it remains low by historical levels, frustrating some buyers.

Brian Burt's biggest complaint is the lack of attractive homes on the market in Scottsdale, Ariz. "They're pretty few and far between," said the 40-year-old lawyer. He recently narrowed his search to homes that don't need any renovations. "When you're looking at rates hovering around 5%, it really takes a bite out of your buying power," he said.

At the same time, prices have ramped up so fast that investors, whose purchases helped ignite the recovery, have pulled back.

Nationally, the share of investors buying homes was 18% in July, down from 20% one year earlier, according to the Campbell Housing Pulse Survey.

MANSION --- Jumbo Jungle: When Appraisal Hassles Tank a Home Sale
By Anya Martin
751 words
13 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
M3
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

In hot markets where bidding wars are common, the property-appraisal process has been short-circuiting transactions, spurring some would-be buyers to walk away.

A few forces are at work. First, many homes are being appraised at a lower price than what buyers have offered on a house. That is because home values are improving so fast in competitive, high-priced markets that "comps," recent sales of nearby homes with comparable characteristics, haven't kept up. When the appraisal comes in low, the buyers either lose the loan or must quickly make up the difference with a higher down payment.

The impact can be significant, said Peter Grabel, a senior mortgage loan originator at Stamford, Conn.-based Luxury Mortgage. A jumbo borrower required to put 20% down on a $1 million purchase expects to pay $200,000. But if the home appraises at $900,000, the down payment goes up to $280,000 -- the sum of the $100,000 shortfall plus the $180,000 down payment.

A second deal-breaker can be the length of time it takes to get an appraisal. "I had one buyer call up and say 'I'm dying a slow, painful death waiting for the appraisal to come in,'" said Sara Benson, president of Chicago-based Benson Stanley Realty who specializes in luxury homes. "I'd never heard that before."

Appraisals of luxury properties tend to be more complex and results may not be ready until as many as 20 days into the loan process, said Allen Cravello, president of El Segundo, Calif.-based mortgage lender and broker American Capital Corp.

To help predict appraisal issues, Mr. Cravello said he runs the home value through an online tool called Cloud Real Estate Network, developed by Denver-based EMC2 Data. It uses Multiple Listing Service data, generally only available to real-estate professionals, to analyze comparable properties.

Delays can also mean losing an interest-rate lock, particularly risky with anticipated rate increases. For example, between May 24 and June 28, rates on a 30-year, fixed-rate jumbo mortgage rose almost a full percentage point from 3.98% to 4.72%, according to HSH.com, a mortgage-information website.

A third force scuttling some transactions is the mortgage-contingency clause in many contracts. That clause allows buyers to walk away from a deal without penalty if they can't get a mortgage; the contingency includes loans that fall through because of a low appraisal. In hot markets, many sellers won't accept an offer that comes with the mortgage contingency -- even if the buyers are preapproved.

Hana Karar, 31, a senior producer at ABC News, and her husband, Kashif Zafar, 33, head of e-book sales for Barnes & Noble Nook, have been saving for the past three years to buy a New York condo. "We need more space and wanted to upgrade; plus, the amount we would have to pay in rent was exceeding what a mortgage payment would be so it made sense," Ms. Karar said in an email.

At multiple open houses, seller's agents told the couple that their offers wouldn't be considered if they didn't waive the mortgage-contingency clause. In another case, a two-bedroom, 1 1/2-bathroom co-op apartment in Brooklyn's Cobble Hill was priced at $859,000. Mr. Zafar and Ms. Karar were encouraged to make an offer and bid $975,000, but the sellers accepted a lower bid that had no mortgage contingency, Ms. Karar said.

Here a few more appraisal considerations:

-- Proactive appraisals. If borrowers suspect appraiser error, they can also request an additional appraisal from a lender, file a rebuttal letter in the slim chance the appraisal will be overturned, or switch lenders to get a new appraisal.

-- Prescreen the appraiser. Ms. Benson recently demanded a lender reassign an appraiser for a $2 million downtown Chicago cooperative after discovering that the appraiser was an uncertified trainee from an office an hour away from the property who had never appraised a cooperative before.

-- Share info. Federal regulations prevent a lender from interacting directly with the appraiser, but those rules don't prohibit a real-estate agent from providing relevant information about the property, such as upgrades, special features, architect name or neighborhood characteristics, said Michael Vargas, president of New York-based Vanderbilt Appraisal Co., which specializes in high-end homes.

MANSION --- It Takes A (Resort) Village --- Once struggling, golf and ski clubs are re-branding themselves as kid-friendly camps for the whole family
By Lauren Schuker Blum and Candace Jackson
1834 words
13 September 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
M1
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

What attracts this generation of wealthy second-home buyers: zip lines, kids clubs and grilled cheese sandwiches.

For decades focused on golf, skiing or hunting, today's master-planned resorts are being reimagined as fancy family camps, a trend that's helping revive a number of struggling high-end second-home communities. Developers say they're aiming to please everyone from toddlers to great-grandparents by merging luxurious trappings with amenities like bowling alleys, soda shops and video arcades. It's all designed to woo wealthy buyers on the hunt for a perfect family vacation.

"These days, you can't just offer a Jack Nicklaus golf course and instantly be successful," says Chris Fair, president of Resonance Consultancy, which advises developers of destination homes and resort communities.

The shift comes after several difficult years in some second-home markets. Residential resorts made up some of the hardest hit real estate during the downturn: Some residential golf communities sold lots for $1.

Meanwhile, interest in kid-focused activities has boomed. There's a "child-centricity" in families that didn't exist in previous generations, says Jon Last, president of Sports & Leisure Research Group, which provides customized research to marketing professionals. Fathers have nearly tripled the amount of time they spend with children, from 2.5 hours a week in 1965 to 7.3 hours per week in 2011, according to a recently released Pew Research report that analyzed years of time-use data. Also, as people are living longer and traveling much later in their lives, vacationing with both parents and grandchildren has become more commonplace.

The first to capitalize were luxury hotel chains like the Ritz-Carlton, which turned some of its properties into five-star playgrounds, complete with game rooms, movie nights and kids classes on everything from marine biology to pastry-making. Ritz-Carlton launched its first major kids programs in the early 1990s, and have expanded them greatly since, according a spokeswoman. "As hotels began offering more of these amenities, buyers began expecting them more when they were on vacation," says Mr. Fair.

When it opened to members in 2000, the Yellowstone Club in Montana was touted as one of the most exclusive clubs in the country, boasting 13,600 acres with private skiing and golf, and members like Bill Gates and former Vice President Dan Quayle. It certainly remains among the more expensive: Although prices are down about 30% from their prerecession peak, according to real-estate agent Tim Murphy, home sites currently begin at $1.5 million, with single-family homes starting at $3.5 million and climbing up to $26.5 million for a full ranch. In addition, owners must pay $32,000 in annual membership dues and a $300,000 refundable deposit upon joining.

In 2008, the club filed for bankruptcy protection. The following year, a group of investors bought the club for $115 million in a bankruptcy auction. One of those investors, Discovery Land Co., an Arizona-based real-estate developer that took over the club's operations in 2009, installed a bevy of family-friendly amenities, including a "20 Below" arena that includes a climbing wall, movie theater, videogame area and a diner with kid-friendly fare like grilled-cheese sandwiches. It created a club for younger children with baby sitting and installed "sugar shacks" -- small cabins stocked with candy and treats -- all over the mountain.

The strategy appears to be working. Last year the club added 35 members compared with 10 in 2009. By the end of the summer of 2013, it had added 45 new members to its ranks.

This year, Nick Woodman, the newly minted billionaire who founded and now is chief executive of digital-camera company GoPro, and his wife bought 34 acres along a ridgeline with a 10,000-square-foot furnished Montana-style home at the Yellowstone Club. "It was really the family-oriented nature of the club that made it a no-brainer for us," says Mr. Woodman, who has two young children. Although he declined to comment on the purchase price, people familiar with the club's real estate estimate that Mr. Woodman spent in excess of $20 million on the home and its land.

While a kid-friendly atmosphere may appeal to some buyers, it also poses a challenge for the resort: making sure that the family focus doesn't turn off a more traditional skiing and golf constituency that is used to exclusive amenities aimed at adults. At Yellowstone, the golf course doesn't have a strict dress code, and there are no tee times, giving the course a more casual feel. But that approach can sometimes backfire. Mr. Woodman says that the club's family-friendly attitude means that it often gets "a bad rap" from the skiing community for being "too easy" of a mountain. "But nobody who actually skis it thinks that," he says. The Yellowstone Club's Director of Sales Bill Collins says the club offers skiing for all levels, from beginners up to the most advanced.

Pine Creek Sporting Club, a private resort community in Okeechobee, Fla., was originally conceived around 2006 as a traditional hunting club with 23 ranch properties of 40 acres each and amenities including a clubhouse, dog kennels and shooting facilities. But by the time developers broke ground in late 2007, in the midst of the economic downturn, they decided to re-brand the property as a 2,400-acre family-centric resort with both ranch properties and smaller cabins, with hunting as just one of many activities available.

Today, the club has a swimming pool and spa, a fitness center and a 600-foot-long zip line that runs across a small lake. There are overnight camping trips and, soon, an Outdoor Pursuits center for kids. A clubhouse wall is lined with photos of member families, and a library is being converted into a game room with coolers that will stock soft drinks and juice boxes. John Reynolds, director of sales and marketing, says about 40% of members are hunters. The other 60% are attracted to the outdoorsy, family-friendly activities. Smaller cabins start at $700,000 and larger ranches, each with 40 acres, range from $2 million to $5 million. The $22,500 annual dues cover most of the amenities and activities, including overnight camping trips for kids (spa treatments, some overnight trips and restaurant meals are additional).

Earlier this year, Karin Taylor, a full-time mom from Palm Beach, Fla., bought a 5,600-square-foot home on 40 acres at Pine Creek with her husband, who runs an industrial buying group. "We really see this as where our kids will have a lot of family memories," says Ms. Taylor, who has five children ranging in age from 3 to 10. "We really weren't interested in being part of a place that didn't welcome the kids." On a recent afternoon, the kids got a hunting-dog demonstration and played in a playground area near a large tree house. Ms. Taylor says they spent about $2 million on their ranch.

At Martis Camp -- a 2,177-acre resort near the north shore of Lake Tahoe that developer DMB/Highlands Group started in 2006 -- amenities aimed at families have helped to ramp up sales in recent years, says Brian Hull, the club's director of sales. There is a family barn that features a bowling alley, a 44-seat movie theater, a swimming area, soda fountain, indoor basketball courts and an outdoor amphitheater. A soccer field, a softball diamond and a barbecue area are nearby. And for the artistically inclined, there's a "folk school" with classes in photography and pottery.

Last year, the club sold 117 properties for $115 million, a step up from the 73 properties it sold in 2010 for $72 million. So far this year, the developer has sold 103 properties for $128 million.

BJ Jenkins, chief executive of computer security and storage company Barracuda Networks, and his wife purchased a 3,250-square-foot modern ski chalet-style home with four bedrooms at Martis Camp last year. The home has become "a gathering place for the entire family," says Mr. Jenkins, who recently had 14 family members stay at his house. "When we get there, we just relax." The couple paid about $3 million for the property, according to people familiar with Martis Camp.

As golfers have been replaced by families, developers are starting to restructure their fees. At Martis Camp, regular members pay an annual fee of $8,900 a year, but golf memberships cost $15,400. At Palmetto Bluff, a 20,000-acre club in Bluffton, S.C., developers recently introduced a lower-priced "social golf" membership at $2,500 per year that includes just 12 rounds of golf. Full-fledged golf members pay a $75,000 refundable deposit upfront, and then $5,500 per year in dues. All homeowners pay $6,650 per year in fees, which includes access to hiking trails, use of kayaks, the fitness center, bike rentals and other activities.

Some communities are also rethinking their architecture. At Yellowstone, a more contemporary look is taking hold, replacing the old mountain style log-and-stone home. The club is planning a renovation of its golf clubhouse with the architect who built the most contemporary residential house on the property -- a 10,000-square-foot structure for new members Steve Taylor, a Las Vegas-based entrepreneur, and his wife, Sascha. The Taylors spent about $10 million to build their home, which has travertine floors, 25-foot-tall ceilings, and 40-foot-tall glass windows.

"Nobody else here has the percentage of glass that we do," says Mr. Taylor while sitting in his living room, which is decorated with sleek black couches and petrified, polished wood stumps from Indonesia. "It's definitely a different look for Yellowstone, but people here have been very supportive of it." Ms. Taylor says that she and her husband are looking forward to raising their daughter, Isabelle, in the club's friendly, social atmosphere.

Meanwhile, Discovery Land Co., which operates Yellowstone, has more family-centered resorts planned, including an 82-lot private golf club in Quogue, N.Y., and a 260-unit property in Millbrook, N.Y., with a golf course, equestrian center and farm.

Michael Meldman, the company's founder and chief executive, says he got the idea to focus on kids' activities from personal experience. "I became a single parent when my kids were very young and I was always trying to create fun, sporty experiences for them without a huge hassle," he says. "My philosophy is that the one thing people can't get more of is time with their families. So that's what we offer."
